That chapter is so heartbreaking that I had to take breaks just to get through reading it.<br /><br />The other is the chapter about Tommy in jail. Instead, they openly despise each other, yet continue to workin the band together because it is the only thing that seems to work for them.<br /><br />It's amazing that after the success they had, some band members could still wind up broke, usually due to a divorce. B00E5Q1LTY|3.0|\\"Star Trek Into Darkness\\" is a bunch of grand performances in search of an actual script.<br />That the plot has holes large enough to accommodate an exploding supernova should come as no surprise: the screenwriters, Roberto Orci, Alex Kurtzman and Damon Lindelof have a long history of substituting tropes, non sequiturs and jarring plot shifts, in place of coherence, character development and tight plotting.<br /><br />This was tolerable in the first JJ Abrams 'Trek' film, because so much of it was fresh and interesting. However, \\"...Into Darkness\\" plays like a bunch of fanboys throwing their fave Trek scenes into a hat, pulling them out randomly, pasting them together and calling it a script.<br /><br />We have 'Khan', the greatest Trek villain of them all...and, one of the greatest in SciFi history...or, do we? The role is miscast with the extraordinary actor Benedict Cumberbatch (BBC's 'Sherlock'). Do I believe that he is brilliant...ruthless...superior? Absolutely. Do I believe him to be Khan Noonian Singh? Not for a moment...he is too elegant to be believed as the ultimate, genetically engineered warlord. What's worse, Khan's marvelous backstory is hardly mentioned...and, is only used as a plot device to give Leonard Nimoy an absolutely unnecessary cameo.<br /><br />The laziness/sloppiness of the 'screenwriting'reaches its nadir, when the \\"...enter the radiation-filled chamber/save the ship/die a hero's death/miraculously come back from the dead...\\" routine from 'Wrath of Khan' is replayed...but, with Kirk and Spock switching roles. The shark has been well and truly jumped.<br /><br />Finally, we have a lazily contrived series of events, starting with an Away Team adventure that seems more Bowery Boys, than Star Trek. These implausible blunders are supposed to make the audience believe that Kirk is a moronic, purile egotist...demanding to go on a black ops mission in order to avenge a death...AND, that the Admiral in charge of Star Fleet (a mis-used Peter Weller)would sanction it without a 2nd thought. Its hard enough to believe that Kirk would (even out of a need for revenge) risk his ship and crew this way. That the crew would blindly follow him on such an insane (asinine) mission is pure tripe. The movie reaches Ludicrous Speed, when we are asked to believe that a massive ship (and spacedock) could be constructed by this Admiral, without anyone at Star Fleet being the wiser. The theme of building tensions and political backroom skullduggery between the Klingons and Star Fleet, drove many excellent episodes of the Trek canon...and, would have made an interesting movie. So, also, would have a new approach to the Khan/Kirk dynamic.<br /><br />Instead, the mashing of the two creates dual, under-developed plot lines that never mesh...and, its a pity, because the acting is simply superb.<br /><br />The cast of this latest Star Trek incarnation lays full claim to their iconic roles in \\"...Into Darkness\\", with the exception the (normally) excellent Simon Pegg as 'Scotty'. His borderline goofiness of the first film has gone over to ham-fisted mugging...for which director Abrams must take a large part of the blame.<br /><br />Zachery Quinto has taken Spock fully into the realm of having/acknowledging emotions. I don't cotton to this version of Mr. Spock...it eliminates the constant struggle with his dual nature; but, Quinto does such a superb job navigating the nuances, that you can't help but like the character. Chris Pine's interpretation of Captain Kirk is the highlight in this movie. Despite the contrivances that the script forces him into, Pine rises above it, and makes the role of Kirk his own. This IS a Captain that a crew would follow, fearlessly on a 5-year mission into Space. The scenes between Pine and Bruce Greenwood (Pike) are marvelous expressions of mentor/student, father/son dynamics...and, completely organic.<br /><br />I was also very disappointed in Michael Giacchino's score. It is (literally) the same score from the previous Trek film, with the Romulan leitmotifs being recycled for Khan, and the Klingons (sounds like a garage band).<br /><br />Bottom line: there is too much to like in \\"Star Trek Into Darkness\\" to allow me to dismiss it out-of-hand. The cast is on-board, and ready for action...however, the director, screenwriters and composer are all on cruise control.<br /><br />That's it...I'm off the bloody soapbox.

B000002HJD|4.0|Even the Doors old producer at the time Paul Rothchild, complained that this was the ultimate abomination upon the legacy and memory of Jim Morrison, I feel a bit differently however, and think that this was an inspired and unique way of paying homage to Jim and his important contributions to popular music of its time. While this release, I don't think, managed to win any new fans of the band, it certainly has awarded the remaining Doors another notch of existing creativity to wet their beaks with. This release continues to divide critics as to its validity of being a true artistic accomplishment or a shameful cash-in on the Doors' and Morrison's name. I find it a rewarding experience and granted, some of the musical accompaniments and arrangements may not necessarily have been what Jim had in mind to correlate to his poetry readings which were recorded during his birthday in late 1970, but, overall, An American Prayer is a worthwhile experience for the Doors fanatic. The inclusion of a live version of Roadhouse Blues is no doubt, an incentive to owning this release, but it seems to flow well into the overall sequencing of the record itself and some of Morrison's pieces like The Movie segment on into Lament and closing with the remaining Doors' interpretation of 16th century Italian composer Tomaso Albinoni's Adagio piece makes it all the more wanting in a definitive statement from the Doors as a whole. The since updated remaster includes a few bonus songs in the forms of Bird of Prey and The Ghost Song which further add relevance and are nice inclusions to the original release. Recommended for the serious Doors fan. Casuals may not be as impressed, but it all depends on your interest level.

B00DL47RQ2|4.0|Woody Allen's 45th film takes him away from his recent foray's to Europe and plants his cast in San Francisco with extended flashback scenes in New York.  While the situation is hardly funny, there are enough elements to certainly put the film in the category of dark comedy.  Jasmine (Cate Blanchett) is the wife of Hal (Alec Baldwin), a wealthy beyond description, investment banker who just so happens to be a white collar crook, unbeknownst to Jasmine.  She is the woman who knows how to live the good life but pays no attention to what's going on around her, especially when it comes to business.  Her business is shopping and hosting elaborate dinner parties.<br /><br />We find much of this out as Jasmine visits her sister, now single Ginger (Sally Hawkins) and her two bratty sons.  Each make a big point out of the fact that they are not biological sisters.  Both were adopted and raised by the same family, but Ginger always says, Jasmine has the better genes.  Ginger, by Jasmine's account has the worst taste in men beginning with her husband Augie who is in construction.  He's played surprisingly well by Andrew Dice Clay.  Upon Jasmine's arrival, Ginger is about to get engaged to Chili, a mechanic played by Bobby Cannavale.<br /><br />Both men are a little rough around the edges and certainly the antithesis of smooth and sophisticated Hal.  But yeah, Hal's a crook.  Not only that, he's a womanizer and everyone knew, except for Jasmine.  Or maybe she knew and just looked the other way.  Enough was enough and in a fit of rage Jasmine, pulls the plug on Hal's business shenanigans.  Jasmine had no idea that the government would strip her of all of her wealth as well.<br /><br />Allen turns Blanchett loose on her character and she is amazing, going from a beautiful socialite concerned about appearances and her next visit to the Mediterranean to a groveling drunk on high doses of Xanax.  A woman with no visible skills, no money and no idea of how to proceed, she's a train wreck.  You'll feel sorry for her by the end of the film.  While Blanchett's performance is sure to get Oscar's attention, the rest of the film bogs down much too often, going over some of the same territory.  While I thought Clay's performance was an eye opener, Louis C. K.'s as a married man having a brief fling with Ginger, wasn't.  Overall this is a film worth seeing, if for no other reason, Cate Blanchett's remarkable performance.reck.  You'll feel sorry for her by the end of the film. But late one night as he's searching for said annoying brother, he falls into a ravine... and wakes up eight years later to discover that everyone and everything except him has aged during those missing years.  When a group of scientists discovers that not only has he not aged, but his brain contains starmaps and schematics that make him an invaluable asset, they threaten to keep him indefinitely.  Understandably freaked out by this, David escapes, chances up on a UFO that was captured around the same time he woke up, and climbs aboard.  There he befriends the ship's logical but curious computer system, Max, and when he discovers Max is responsible for his predicament, he helps the UFO escape and tries to return home.<br /><br />The concept of this movie, while it's been done before in literature (think of it as a futuristic Rip Van Winkle), is fairly unique to film, which adds to this film's charm.  It has a realism and earnestness to it that most modern sci-fi films lack, partly due to the strong performances by the various actors but also due to the realism of the special effects.  With CGI technology not quite up to par yet, this film works best when it relies on puppetry and animatronics to bring its alien characters to life, and both Max and the alien specimens aboard his ship are all the more believable and impressive for it.<br /><br />If I have a nitpick with this movie, it's with Max.  Voiced by Paul Reuben, better known as Pee-Wee Herman, he works best when he's his usual logical yet curious self.  But partway through the film, thanks to an accidental mind-meld with David, he becomes pretty much a robotic Pee-Wee, cracking obnoxious jokes and speaking with an annoying nasal voice that's just a deeper version of Pee-Wee's.  It's not enough to make me hate the film, but it does get grating enough to knock it down a star.<br /><br />All in all, despite the dated CGI and obnoxious Pee-Wee impersonation, this is an underrated classic of the '80s, and worth a look for sci-fi fans young and old.  Good for kids 8 and up.o it that most modern sci-fi films lack, partly due to the strong performances by the various actors but also due to the realism of the special effects. Every relationship is rushed, with characters instantly falling in love with each other or deciding they hate someone and are now sworn enemies after only exchanging a glance. Characters remain flat, even by the 3rd book, which is when I could no longer keep reading. There is such heavy-handed foreshadowing that every \\"twist\\" could be seen coming by any child capable of reading.<br /><br />The thing that bothered me the most is that the author seems to just google names for the characters. For instance, the evil king of the neighboring country has a last name that's just a bastardized form of the protagonist's last name. This might be on purpose, but if so, again with the heavy-handed foreshadowing. Even more laughable, a woman was given the name 'Alistair', which is a male's name. After she told a male character this, he responded with \\"that's the most beautiful name I've ever heard.\\" I wanted to throw the book at the wall. But maybe this is foreshadowing too? Will she turn out to be a guy?<br /><br />To sum up, if the author learns how to flesh out his/her characters and make them multidimensional, if he/she takes more time to develop relationships between characters such that it seems believable, and gets a good editor to find and fix all the glaring errors that break immersion and frustrate the reader, this might actually be a good series. Then, this might deserve 3 or 4 stars, but as it stands, it only deserves 2.

B008GGA5EQ|5.0|Thurlow's work never ceases to amaze and surprise me. I always get deeply engaged in her stories, even when she writes about the type of sex that doesn't usually interest me--a testament to her skill as a storyteller and a seductress. Beautiful writing, well-developed characters and intriguing plots are three reasons why I keep coming back for more.<br /><br />Chloe Thurlow is gifted writer; a modern day Anais Nin. She weaves erotica into something that transcends sex.<br /><br />I don't want to give away the plot, that would spoil the fun, so here's a little tease: Girl Trade delves into the exotic world of tribal life and the sex trade. It's about reinventing yourself and taking an unflinching look at who you really are underneath all the artifice. Erotic love is explored on many different levels. It isn't meant as a road map to life, far from it. I see the story as a metaphor for how women have locked their sexual impulses away in a box for too long. We've been so busy trying to be good girls that we forgot to honor our primal instincts and our divine feminine.<br /><br />By reading the negative reviews, you'll gather that this book is not for everyone. But if you are a wild child, a sexual adventurer or a passionate soul, you will find something marvelous in Girl Trade. You might blush, laugh, cry or get turned on, but more importantly you'll learn something about yourself and that's the best reason of all to read it.

Some reviewers have called the ending unbelievable or contrived, but I know of true events that parallel this tale. I would recommend to a reader who has some knowledge of rural Ireland - not the shamrock and leprechaun land, but the real country.

B00BVMXBVG|5.0|Lately, there have been a lot of shows  using the device of twins borrowing identities. Perhaps it's a trend, perhaps it's just a fan fave, as Shakespeare himself went for it in Comedy of Errors and Twelfth Night. In any case, Orphan Black up the ante. Not twins or triplets, but human clones. And there are at least seven of them. Plus, it's better than last years duplicate thrillers: Ringer (as much as i'm a SMG fan) and The Lying Game (which is pretty good in a lightweight way).<br /><br />What is crazy impressive about Orphan is that Canadian actress Titiana Maslany (Shakespeare reference right there in her name!) manages to pull off wildly distinct personalities was incredible aplomb. I mean, seriously, you can just feel the different presence of these girls. You can even see quite effectively when one of them is pretending to be another. Watching uptight suburban mom Allison pretending to be punk Sarah is hilarious -- and effective.<br /><br />This show is Science Fiction, but the SF is confined to the clone thing and it's low budget too. There aren't a ton of effects. What there is, however, is very good writing, casting, acting, and pacing. It's a great show really. We can't know that the quality will survive into later seasons, but this one is break neck. The characters are sympathetic and interesting, and boy is she(s) put through the ringer (sorry SMG). The tone is simultaneously dark and comic, but always tense and unpredictable. There is one bit in the pilot where Sarah (playing Beth) is caught in an impossible situation. She goes to the bathroom to buy herself some time, then does something completely unexpected that actually works as a clever solution. This is very effective thriller plotting. It doesn't feel forced or overwrought but merely tense.<br /><br />Importantly, the complex central mystery is drooled out episode by episode, but it is drooled out. We find out quite a lot -- although hardly everything. The amount of reveal is very effective in this season, but could be problematic next year as they will not be able to depend on the same  dynamic. The show might not be able to depend on the clones even playing their normal lives (or each others).<br /><br />While Maslany steals the show. Many of the secondary actors are very solid as well. Jordan Gavaris as her (very) gay foster brother is a standout. He about says it all when he sits down at a piano and says, \\"let me show you a bit of Queen.\\" Kevin Hanchard is solid as cop twin Beth's partner.<br /><br />The show feels slightly schizophrenic with regard to its sexuality. At times, in some episodes (like the pilot), it's pleasantly steamy. Maslany does a good bit of walking around in underwear (always a plus). But this remains uneven, unpredictable. When two characters close we never know if the camera will linger or cut to morning. In a way, this keeps the viewer off balance. Deliberate thriller style or mere inconsistency? Who knows.<br /><br />Either way, the show is very much worth watching.<br /><br />Andy Gavin, author of Untimed and The Darkening Dreamhis season, but could be problematic next year as they will not be able to depend on the same  dynamic. 0425267695|4.0|Structurally, this book borrows heavily from The Name of The Wind. It opens with a box story about a famous military man and then slowly dolls out the (first) chapters of his long career, beginning with his schooling. Blood Song is well written, with solid practical prose that doesn't get in the way. There is none of the elegant and overwrought voice of the aforementioned Rothfus, or the descriptive nuance of Martin, but it's well written.<br /><br />Even though the scope is big, this is a more focused story than A Song of Ice and Fire or A Wheel of Time. We follow our single hero fairly tightly (even if his life meanders). Except for the occasional return to the frame story there are no other points of view. Al Sorna, our protagonist, rises within a kind of military-religious brotherhood perhaps most akin to the Knights Templar. The world building is very solid and the author clearly knows something about the late medieval period. There are several religions and nations and they clash in a fairly realistic way.<br /><br />The overall effect is one I'm still processing. I liked the book. A lot. It's one of the better epic fantasies I've read lately (and that is my favorite genre). The first third is great, during the youth and training of our hero. Some of the characters are excellent. My biggest problem is from about the 50-94% point. Here Al Sorna is commander of a big military expedition and the narrative became a little harder to follow. It's not that I couldn't tell what was going on from scene to scene, but they didn't fit together seamlessly. Unlike the earlier sequences, they also didn't seem to have the weight that I think the author was intending. There is similar stuff in The Name of The Wind (not so military), but it resonates much more emotionally in that novel.<br /><br />I'll explain what I mean. Al Sorna has this \\"unrequited love of his life\\" (just like Kvothe and Denna in TNOTW), but their interactions, while fine, lack the heavy sense of tragedy of Rothfus' superior novel. It's not bad, but it just comes off a little weak.<br /><br />The  end of the book is good. There are two big \\"fights.\\" But the sequencing felt a bit disconnected. And that's basically the thing with this novel. It needs editing. The parts are good, but the sum doesn't reach greatness.<br /><br />As an after-note, I'm a little mystified as to how this book has such incredible ratings on Amazon. At this writing: 1003 total, 863 5-star, 108 4-star, 22 3-star, 7 2-star, and 3 1-star. This is very very slanted toward 4-5 star. Now, it's got enough good stuff going for it to be a 4 star novel, and epic fantasy unfortunately is full of some serious duds. But an average of 4.8? This is higher than A Game of Thrones which is the best series start in the last 20 years. I can't help but wonder what weird factor is going on here.<br /><br />Andy Gavin, author of Untimed and The Darkening DreamRothfus' superior novel. 0780619587|5.0|Based on a true biography of a father who instills a love of music (being especially partial to Rachmaninov) into his children, particularly his gifted son, David Helfgott. After David comes to America, a professor, realizing his virtuosos, develops an overarching ambition to do whatever it took to ensure his student reaching his full potential as a pianist. During this time, David is also beginning to manifest symptoms of a psychological disorder. B00DHHWYAW|4.0|My favorite film of 2013, the severely underrated, The Great Gatsby finally arrives on Blu-ray 3D this week in a nice, full featured set.<br /><br />Movie<br /><br />I don't think I've ever read The Great Gatsby. I know it is required reading in a lot of High School English Lit classes, but I'm fairly certain, surprisingly enough, that I didn't take English Lit - not even in College (I took French and Spanish instead). Always the practical one, who knew that I'd end up being \\"forced\\" to read all the classics anyway?<br /><br />After a string of misfires (I hated Shutter Island and Inception) Leonardo DiCaprio returns to form. He is perfect as Gatsby. He deserves an Oscar nomination for this performance. Gatsby is a complicated, yet simple man. His entire existence is his desire to get the girl, Daisy (Carey Mulligan) that he foolishly let go of.<br /><br />This is a role that was tailor made for DiCaprio. If DiCaprio wasn't already an A list mega star, this movie would have made him one. He just oozes off the screen and is simply radiant here (can I use the word radiant to talk about a guy?).<br /><br />In any other hands, Daisy could easily be hated and come across as shallow, naive, and as someone just going through life, but Mulligan brings a lot of hidden layers to Daisy. You can see the inner conflict through her expressive eyes.<br /><br />As wild and over the top as Moulin Rouge Director Baz Luhrmann usually is; The Great Gatsby is his most assured, straightforward, gimmick free (not counting 3D) film to date. From a pure story telling and directing standpoint it is by the numbers. There are very few scenes that made me feel like I took a bad acid trip. Even the party scenes are beautifully shot and framed here.<br /><br />Much has been written about the modern mixing of Hip Hop with 20's era Jazz and how it wouldn't work. Luhrmann actually did an amazing job of not overdoing the gimmick. Yes, it is noticeable, but he uses it very sparingly, so it never tookme out of the movie.<br /><br />There was one scene when they were on the bridge and they passed a car that had rich black people in and they were playing Jay-Z's Izzo (HOVA). It was odd and random just to have rich black people appear out of nowhere. I loved Luhrmann use of various versions Lana Del Rey's song Young and Beautiful in the love scenes. It really added texture and emotion to the romance.<br /><br />One of the most beautiful moments in the movie comes when Gatsby is lamenting everything that he's lost, and Tobey Maguire (Nick Carraway) says, \\"You can't repeat the past\\" and Gatsby responds with an earnest \\"Of course you can old sport,\\" such a simple but impactful moment.<br /><br />Generally, I'm not a fan of narration, but Maguire does an amazing job with the lyrical prose used in this movie. Maguire gives a mature, nuanced performance here where you can really see his love and infatuation for Gatsby grow while his distaste for his friend Tom Buchannan (a wonderfully evil Joel Edgerton) increases. At times it almost seemed as if Nick wanted to jump Gatsby's bones.<br /><br />This is my favorite film of 2013. It hit all my buttons - beautifully shot, very hopeful (but with an ultimately sad end), and had some flashes of brilliant prose.<br /><br />Video/Audio/Packaging<br /><br />This movie received a lot of flack because it was pulled from its original release date and delayed by 6 months so that Luhrmann could add 3D. I'm here to tell you the 3D really doesn't ad much to this film, yeah there is some good depth to it, but it seems like a gimmick and pretty pointless. I preferred the regular 2D Blu-ray version.<br /><br />The quality on the Blu-ray 3D is solid, if I'm looking directly at it, however if I lay down on my couch it become basically unwatchable. Lack of viewing angles is one of the drawbacks to using Passive 3D technology, but normally they aren't this off.<br /><br />In this day and age, can we get rid of letterboxing? Please? I have a large screen television for a reason; I don't want half my screen taken up with black bars. The Blu-ray 3D would have been more eye-popping if it used full screen. Other than the letterboxing issue, the transfer is almost flawless. There are a couple of scenes that purposefully has some grain to help establish the time period.<br /><br />At $29 (Amazon price) this is the most \\"expensive\\" Blu-ray 3D set that I've purchased all year and the packaging is cheap as heck. There are three discs and the Blu-ray and Blu-ray 3D discs don't even have individual holders - just two discs slapped together.<br /><br />There's no artwork on the two Blu-ray disks, just Gatsby written on it in a fuggly font. Looks like some amateur bootleg disk, not a professional $30 Blu-ray 3D set. What was WHV thinking? Oh, I know, \\"We'll do a better job with an extended version in a few months.\\"<br /><br />Extras<br /><br />The Extras on this set are nicely laid out and contain a lot of solid information about every aspect of creating this movie. I kind of wish there was just one full length documentary (broken into individual featurettes), because it felt like I was hunting and pecking through the menu.<br /><br />It was weird that the Blu-ray 3D trailer is on the Blu-ray disc, but not the actual Blu-ray 3D itself. I mean what do I get out of seeing an ad for Blu-ray 3D in 2D?<br /><br />There is One Trailer for Gravity<br /><br />1926 The Great Gatsby Trailer (1 min, HD) - This long lost trailer is a fun look at the old silent film classic.<br /><br />Within and Without with Tobey Maguire (8 mins, HD) - Tobey Maguire brings his camera on set for some rare moments with Co-Stars and Director Baz Luhrmann<br /><br />The Swinging Sounds of Gatsby (12 Min, HD) - This feature looks at the amazing, eclectic score that blends the sound of Jazy-Z, Bryan Ferry and other contemporary artists with classic 20s era jazz<br /><br />The Jazz Age (15 Min, HD) - A nice, detailed look at the history of the Jazz Age and the parties that inspired it.<br /><br />Razzle Dazzle: The Fashion of the 20s (15, HD) - This is exactly what the title says, a look at the fashions of the 20s.<br /><br />Fitzgerald's Visual Poetry (7 min, HD) - Baz talks about the importance of using 3D for this film to create \\"poetic glu.\\"<br /><br />Gatsby Revealed (30 Min, HD) - This is essentially a 30 minute documentary (broken up into several pieces) that delves into various aspects of how various scenes were created and staged. Very well done.<br /><br />Deleted Scenes - There are three deleted scenes, one of which includes an Alternate Ending. The Alternate ending is interesting because it shows Gatsby's father coming to pay his respects and there was a romance between Nick and Jordan that unfortunately got cut because Baz didn't have enough time to fully flesh it out.<br /><br />Digital UV Copy - After spending a lot of time complaining about UltraViolet and clutching onto iTunes, I'm now fully onboard and love the idea of it. When UV first started, my primary issue was all the various sign-ups and the fact that I could not play the digital files anywhere. In the last 8 months UV has become almost as ubiquitous as Netflix.I can get UV through Vudu, CinemaNow and Flixter and all those apps are available on every connected device (TVs, Blu-ray Players, Game Consoles) I have.As much as I love iTunes, it annoys me that I had to buy two Apple TVs just to watch my digital copies on my TV.<br /><br />With that said, I hate having a fractured digital library - I have 300 UVs and 100 iTunes now, so I wish studios would provide both UV and iTunes copies. It is also annoying that Digital Copies don't include the extra features and that services like Vudu sell bonus materials separately and that iTunes Extras don't even work on Apple TV.<br /><br />Conclusion<br /><br />This movie is the ultimate love story about a man that would and did do everything he could to gain back what he lost. Having never read the book, I can't comment on what has been changed but it is a movie that everyone should enjoy. This Blu-ray 3D package is disappointing, I'm not sure what I expected, but I wanted more than was delivered. Even so, it is hard to be mad when I love this movie as much as I do. The sloppy packaging, the outrageous price and the annoying letterboxing are the only things holding this back from an A+.<br /><br />Grades<br /><br />Movie - A+<br />Video - B+<br />Audio - B+<br />Extras - C<br /><br />Final Overall Grade - B+

B0002XMKYE|5.0|This magnificent movie is one of my favourites. It is difficult not to fall in love with General Patton. But be careful. It just may be George C. Scott's performance which enamoured you.<br /><br />The account of Patton's record during World War 11 is taken from memoirs of himself, Gen. Omar Bradley and German records. This is the one general the Germans stood in awe of: the one general they feared. When the Germans were burning records and making a retreat during the end of the war, one of them, burning his picture, called Patton the pure warrior, whom the absence of war would kill. Within the next year, Patton was dead, dying from an accident after having come through the war unscathed.<br /><br />Patton was a lot of things. One thing he was not was political. He didn't know how to keep his mouth shut. He said and did disturbing things, getting in hot water continually with Ike (Gen. Dwight D. Eisenhower). He couldn't get away with anything, since he made such good copy, the reporters were always after him.<br /><br />He slapped a soldier and declared him a coward who was shell-shocked (or whatever the modern term is) and whose nerves were totally gone. For that, Ike made him apologize to the soldier, units of soldiers, and the hospital staff. But did he do it on their level? He did not. He stood on a balcony above them, looking down on them. Had he been on the same level, it might have been more effective.<br /><br />He stormed across Europe to Berlin, marching his soldiers 24 hours to relieve the Battle of the Bulge soldiers, then on to Berlin. But still, he couldn't stay his mouth or stay out of trouble. In an interview with reporters, he was asked about leaving Nazis in key positions. He said that they knew how to keep the water works and electricity working. In response to one question, he said that Nazis had joined the party in the same way that American Republicans or Democrats had done. It was in response to a trick question, and he fell for it.<br /><br />He also wanted to kick the Russians in the behind and send them back where they belonged. He told Ike he had the troops and begged to be able to do it. Given the cold war that developed for years and years, Patton was right.<br /><br />But he was politically incorrect. He suffered for it. The Americans got rid of him. In a well-balanced scene, it showed Field Marshall Montgomery of Britain being received by the King of England to be honoured with the country's highest award. The scene switches to that of Patton leaving his post and shaking the hands of every one of his staff.<br /><br />It is heart-rending, but let's keep in mind that Patton's boss (Ike) was looking, even then, towards the presidency. He couldn't afford to have such a politically incorrect general who made such good copy for reporters. So Ike got rid of him.<br /><br />However, it is also possible that, as the Germans predicted, this greatest of warriors had outlived his time and that the absence of war killed him.<br /><br />Patton was a deeply religious man. My favourite scene is when he was on his way to relieve the Battle of the Bulge warriors. It had snowed deeply for days and air cover was needed. Patton sends for the chaplain. He tells him he wants &#34;a weather prayer.&#34; He assures the chaplain that he, Patton, is on such good terms with the Almighty, that if the prayer is offered, the next day will be clear for air cover. Then he says: &#34;I expect that prayer within the hour.&#34;<br /><br />The next day was clear. B00A7KKPDK|5.0|A cousin of mine recently posted a photo of this book on her Instagram account with the caption, \\"I don't usually like to read but this book is awesome! And for a writer, where tone and voice is so important, that just goes to show that Ellen's voice is so, so strong (which got me to thinking about my own voice as a writer and what it sounds like, but that's probably something for another blog post). That distinct voice is actually the key to really enjoying the book, because you find yourself imagining Ellen telling the story to you in that cheeky way of hers. The punctuation (or lack thereof) also makes all the difference when she tells a story.<br /><br />The book is filled with little anecdotes, random musings, and as Ellen herself puts it in her note for the reader, \\"I decided to include a little something for everyone in this book. You're going to find some short stories for adults, coloring pages for kids, and many things for all ages in between.\\" What I love is that in the middle of all the humor --and there is a LOT, since it's Ellen-- there are also many nuggets of wisdom.<br /><br />You don't have to be a fan of Ellen to enjoy this book. It's easy to read, light and humorous, but still has enough wisdom to make it worth your time and money. The title itself, Seriously... I'm Kidding, encapsulates this very balance. So I found myself laughing while reading, at the same time thinking, \\"Wow, Ellen makes a lot of sense!\\"<br /><br />Try to grab a copy of this book, if you can. It's the perfect way to destress after a long day. After all, laughter is the best medicine!<br /><br />(This review can also be found on my blog: [...]

B00DLKXF3A|5.0|I've enjoyed Avenged Sevenfold's albums for at least the past five years and I can confidently say that I am a huge fan. I love their metalcore era, it contained perhaps some of the rawest metal that was on the market at the time with some tight riffs and epic melodies. Then City of Evil came around and introduced a more heavy metal oriented band without screamed vocals and more reliant on musical excellence. This continued through their self-titled record and Nightmare, both which are amazing records in their own rights. I choose to review an album based on it's own merit which is what I will do, I will most likely have to compare it to classic metal legends like Iron Maiden, Judas Priest, Old-Metallica, and Guns N' Roses because that is what this album envelopes. I will just throw that out there right now, I know I'm contradicting myself but it's inevitable. Hail to the King if one of the finest straight-forward metal records I've heard in a long time. From the bands I listed above, Hail to the King is a combination of The Black Album, Use Your Illusion II, Screaming For Vengeance, and Somewhere in Time. This record is tremendous and is perhaps my favorite record by AX7 but only time well tell for that statement is fickle in its' own right. Here's a song by song review, some track picks, etc.<br /><br />1. \\"Shepherd of Fire\\" 10/10 - Has a tingle of For Whom the Bell Tolls and Enter Sandman but the rawness of Avenged Sevenfold. This opening is tremendous with the inclusion of brass instruments and large to create this epic atmosphere. The song itself introduces a very simplistic drum beat but when done correctly, simplicity is perhaps the most original idea that a band can have. This song displays the potential of this record and basically lays down the foundation for the rest of the record.<br /><br />2. \\"Hail to the King\\" 9.5/10 - The intro riff is so reminiscent of an Iron Maiden song, it's incredible how this record just soars back through time with the intention of introducing an old style with a new sound. This song is the first single, I was fairly impressed with the direction when I first heard it and since then, I've been constantly listening to it. The chorus, my god, replace it with Bruce Dickinson's vocals and you got Iron Maiden. The guitar solo is quite impressive as well.<br /><br />3. \\"Doing Time\\" 9/10 - Is that Guns N' Roses, sounds like it. What a great song, one of the more upbeat songs on the record, it also has this great scream by M. Shadows. It's a very simple song, very straight-forward, the shortest track on the record. It carries so much weight and energy in three minutes, love this song.<br /><br />4. \\"This Means War\\" 10/10 - Sounds like The Black Album, it's 'sad but true'. The riffs are very groovy, so much to love about this song. I absolutely love the composition, it's so simple but so brilliant. It follows a normal song pattern but the addition and subtractions of instruments help to create this epic feeling when the chorus finally arrives. M. Shadows vocals are very powerful on this track and I particularly love the guitar solo on this track. One of my favorites off the record.<br /><br />5. \\"Requiem\\" 10/10 - Some Latin choir chants, some darker guitar tones, and a very groovy beat. This track is absolutely filthy, awesome song. M. Shadows' vocals are so powerful in the 'chorus', the lyrics are very interesting, it's just a great track. The guitar solo sounds like something Kirk Hammett would play except better. Awesome song!<br /><br />6. \\"Crimson Day\\" 10.5/10 - Time for a ballad, reminds me of Metallica power ballads except with much better vocals. This is a beautiful track, the vocals are really the highlight with some beautiful harmonies. The addition of some violins really pulls on the heart strings and not to mention the epic-beautiful guitar solo. One of my favorites!<br /><br />7. \\"Heretic\\" 8.8/10 - This song is very interesting, I wasn't incredibly impressed with it at first because the riff was almost too simple but when I listened to it again, I respected that riff and what it stood for. I hear so many classic bands in this song, it's such a great song. Surprisingly, it might be the weakest track on the entire record but that means absolutely nothing because it's an awesome song. The guitar absolutely raises this song though, it's quite impressive.<br /><br />8. \\"Coming Home\\" 11/10 - This song is literally enveloping Iron Maiden, it's such an amazing mix of music, vocals, and production. The double guitar riffing is very intense, it has a thrashy like verse, it can even be something that Megadeth wrote in their hey-day. The chorus is absolutely the catchiest on the record, amazing song. One of the best, if not the best!<br /><br />9. \\"Planets\\" 10/10 - Another impressive track, this track and Coming Home are perhaps the heaviest tracks on the record but they still carry such a great classic vibe. M. Shadows' vocals are so strong on this track, it's nice to hear some double bass as well. Love the riffs in this song, I can't say anymore cause I'm running out of adjectives.<br /><br />10. \\"Acid Rain\\" 10.5/10 - A ballad as the last song, a piano the reigns throughout the song, it's quite beautiful. I love some orchestra with my Avenged Sevenfold. The guitar solos are very bluesy, very lovely additions to the song. The chorus displays some great vocal strength from M. Shadows, it's one epic song. A great closing song to an incredible album.<br /><br />I highly recommend this record to anybody who respects music, loves Avenged Sevenfold, and loves classic metal. This record is perhaps the most nostalgic piece of art I've heard since I heard The Darkness' new record. This is going to be on repeat for months. What a tremendous accomplishment! Buy it!

0743497465|3.0|This is a summer book in the sense it's easy to read, pleasantly distracting and leaves nothing on the palate or in the mind once done.<br /><br />Dan Brown, successful as he is, just isn't a great writer.  He like to tell instead of show.  He likes to spoon feed his audience, unable to resist the urge to explain the concept behind what a character just said or did.  He likes to create what I call false drama, ending a chapter with a line like, \\"Little did he know it would be the last vision of his short life,\\" and then apply the brakes with a drier, sometimes unnecessary chapter.  He loves \\"quotations.\\"  Brown does do his research, but it rarely feels truly synthesized into a story. It can feel like reading someone who piled up his Google research and dumped it into a couple pages to elicit the feel of secret knowledge.<br /><br />Deception Point is fun.  Hardly mysterious in its road map, but fun.  The only problem I had - other than Brown's style - was the dogged insistence that NASA is the wedge that divides the country.  It's not really even a point of contention with so many other urgent national matters.  But the drama that unfolds around that premise is interesting, albeit ridiculous at times.<br /><br />One minor point.  Dan Brown uses sharks as one of his villains in this story.  That's fine, except he got their science and behavior almost completely wrong.  That made me wonder about the other \\"science\\" he presented in the story.  If you have a well exercised suspension of disbelief, this won't bother you.<br /><br />There's nothing really wrong about Deception Point.  There's just nothing really right with it, either.

0385345054|5.0|I love paper arts and either dabble or dive into them depending on my schedule, need to indulge, budget, etc, and I've been committing a greater-than-average amount of time into paper arts booka for the last year. I've got a bookcase of some really cute books, some informative, and some works or art that I will never master.  This book has it all, and then some. Photos, text, templates, bound in a really thick book. Not the usual craft book.<br /><br />There is a section devoted to the \\"examples\\" they have made for clients, all 75 flowers are magical.  The next section describes the materials used to make paper flowers. The fact that they don't force any brand is a plus, if you aren't new to paper arts, you already have most of the materials, except, perhaps, for the stems and centers. The skills section is vital unless you are an expert in creating paper flowers. It is really useful that they have picture and text intermixed and that they break down flowers to their smallest parts. (center, stamen, stem, petal, etc) Each technique is introduced \\"contextually\\". So many helpful images of action and steps. Different means of shaping for different paper and a focus on creating rounded, organic forms. Because that is what nature is, correct? Organic, rounded flowers, no matter what colors you use are going to look amazingly real, just like all of the examples in the book, over something straight, flat, and fake. There is none of that in this guide. Not in the images or the writing, or even their style, it is really natural, beautiful and likeable.<br /><br />The how-to section gives you just that for each one of those 75 flowers, broken down to their visual individual parts, and marks the skill level of each one. They are using a scale, of 1,2 or 3,; 1(basic techniques) to 3 (advanced techniques). The authors are so kind as to give us some advice as how someone with a solid 2 level skill could modify a 3 flower project to their strengths, which I find especially helpful, and give you a rubric for mastery at each level. This is something more \\"high-level\\" crafters need to include in their guides; not just beginner techniques, not just advanced techniques or looks, but what does the spectrum look like, what does it include? How can I take a project that is too advanced and target it towards my level (What to remove in the flower? What to add to make the project look good, help me improve skills, but not look like my 3rd grader did it?)<br /><br />After the how-tos, as the other reviewers have stated, there are pages of templates to the shapes used for the flower - well over 200. They include a good source guide. One warning to the paper newbie, some of this paper and related materials can be extremely expensive. You can go through and find the materials that work for you and your project(s) at any price point with the variety of resources the authors include.<br /><br />This will be THE paper (handmade) flower book for the year, if not years. What an excellent job this couple has done on their first book (she was an style director at Martha Stewart Weddings and they run a design business), I look forward to seeing more from them!, and give you a rubric for mastery at each level. This is something more \\"high-level\\" crafters need to include in their guides; not just beginner techniques, not just advanced techniques or looks, but what does the spectrum look like, what does it include? How can I take a project that is too advanced and target it towards my level (What to remove in the flower? What to add to make the project look good, help me improve skills, but not look like my 3rd grader did it?)<br /><br />After the how-tos, as the other reviewers have stated, there are pages of templates to the shapes used for the flower - well over 200. They include a good source guide. One warning to the paper newbie, some of this paper and related materials can be extremely expensive. You can go through and find the materials that work for you and your project(s) at any price point with the variety of resources the authors include.<br /><br />This will be THE paper (handmade) flower book for the year, if not years. B00BUADSMQ|3.0|It is very rare I actually give my opinions on films, I never summarize the plots or give opinions on acting, and almost never buy a Blu Ray without having seen the movie first in one form or another.  I usually only review the quality of the Blu Ray itself as transferred from Standard Def.  Today, I make an exception.  Yes, I heard all the negative remarks, but I am very capable of forming my own opinions without the influence of others.  So, IMHO, this is not a Die Hard film as we have come to know them, but an action film more in the vein of those horrid Transformers films.  Previous Die Hard movies had a great degree of personality in John McClain's character, and strong and visible antagonists who challenged him every step of the way.  This film has neither and has only the slightest hint of a storyline in that Bruce comes to Russia to save his son, who is now a secret CIA operative in battle with a Russian big wig.  That's it.  The entirety of the film is one huge cacophony of stunt work and CGI.  I was very disappointed and think they should have stolen Sly Stallone's movie title and called it 'Die Hard, Over the Top'.  Never the less, my take on the Video and Audio Quality.<br /><br />VIDEO...<br />  Surprised that such a new film on Blu Ray would have this much grain in its image.  Especially against gray backgrounds, the grain crawls to annoyance.  There is a slight bleach bypass to the color grading and a definite crushing of the blacks to maximize the dreariness of the American view of Russia.  With the blacks being crushed as they are, there is also a loss of fine detail.  A smoothing out of skin features also removes the details there, and in some scenes, the characters almost appeared cartoonish.  With a Mbps rate that is relatively high in the low thirties, I was surprised at the comparatively poor video reproduction.  Yes, you can get used to it, and I did.  But why should you have to in the first place?  2.5-3 stars.<br /><br />AUDIO...<br />  We should be thrilled that not only is the audio codec a lossless DTS HD but that you can have it in a 7.1 configuration as well as a 5.1.  I wish more films offered a 7.1 mix but understand that most viewers have a 5.1 surround system at best.<br /> The problem with the audio however is that the mix makes for a cacophony of noise.  Yes, there is a great deal of discreet panning and directionality from all the home theater surrounds, but the overload of noise takes away from the directionality by so immersing the listener that the audio is hard to follow.  As one who would take pristine audio over video any day and loves a great directional mix, I am amazed that I am even saying this.  On a positive note, the LFE channel will earn all the money you spent on it and, I can say, that some scenes actually offer DEMO worthy audio to show off your system with. 4 stars.<br /><br />EXTRAS...You do get the disc on Standard Def as well as a digital copy and you also have the choice between the theatrical release and extended cut.  I based this review upon the extended cut.  Other extras are deleted scenes, trailers, commentaries and other short docs I have not yet viewed.<br /><br />I do hope that this review has been of some help to you in determining your possible future purchase.  All my Blu Ray reviews focus solely upon the quality of the video and audio.  All except this one that is.<br />Thanks for reading.hould be thrilled that not only is the audio codec a lossless DTS HD but that you can have it in a 7.1 configuration as well as a 5.1. 1592402690|3.0|Emerick loses me when he elevates his role in The Beatles story. First, he was there for less than half of their studio output, and has a habit of disparaging the albums he missed out on. I personally don't hear much difference in the sound from Rubber Soul to Revolver. I will admit the tape loops, effects, etc. do add to Revolver and Sgt. Pepper, but I'm not sure if he can claim credit for all of that. The band's influences were changing and expanding which had as much to do with the sounds you hear as the not-so-humble engineer. Would the songs suffer if Norman Smith were engineering Revolver and Pepper? Who knows? His treatment of Martin could be seen as self serving, but some of Martin's recollections also seem self serving. Everyone seems to want to claim credit for the success of The Beatles.<br /><br />In regards to his treatment (mis-treatment?) of the three less-Fab, I must disagree with many of the critics here. I think he was completely fair to all four, and I don't think anything he has written hasn't been said before! The treatment of George was completely in keeping with the history as i've understood it. He talks about how much George grew from the Revolver sessions to Abbey Road. He credits his guitar work (solos) on Abbey Road mentioning how he re-dubbed his solo on 'Something' in one take (because it had to be) while accompanied by classical musicians. This was something that a less confidant George would not have attempted a few years earlier. I'm a George fan and I always believed that was part of his charm, the less talented (more grounded in many ways) little brother who finally came into his own. Somewhere along the way people have raised The Beatles to a mythical proportion, believing that all four were 'geniuses.' They were not. Maybe Lennon and McCartney have some claim. Harrison benefited from being the understudy to two of the greatest song-writers of all time, and he eventually became a decent one himself. He became a damn good guitar player along the way too. Emerick gives him plenty of credit for his growth. Sorry Ringo fans, but Ringo also was not a genius. Hate to break it to you! This rewriting of the Ringo story is contrarianism at it's worst. Ringo hit the lottery with The Beatles. Can anyone honestly with a straight face say The Beatles would have been less successful without Ringo? C'mon! Did 'Back in the USSR' suffer because Ringo was home pouting? Did the White Album sessions come to a halt when he threw his fit? For a not-fantastic drummer to try to quit The Beatles is artistic suicide. Luckily, beneath all their bitchiness, I think the other three were good guys, and liked Ringo. Otherwise he'd be off with Pete Best wondering what might have been. Much lesser bands wouldn't have allowed Ringo within 30 feet of a microphone. He's made a nice career living off his Beatle fame and has become something of a clown prince of Rock, but save me the Ringo is underrated nonsense. I did like 'Octopuses' Garden' though. Anyone who doesn't know that Lennon went through a passive psychedelic stage and emerged angry and aggressive isn't a Beatles fan. It's also no knock to say he wasn't a great guitar player. This is nothing new from Emerick. He gives him plenty of credit for his singing and songwriting. These were his strengths. It has been stated elsewhere that Paul was responsible for many of the innovations The Beatles were responsible for. Somewhere along the way the Lennonites have tried to turn John into some sort of avant-garde genius. His genius again was his songwriting. To say Paul helped Lennon's songs out more than Lennon helped Paul's, to me is stating the obvious. Paul was much more interested, patient, etc. when it came to making a track. This has also been used as an example of an annoying quality of his (Obla-Di-Obla-Da). Emerick stating this is hardly a revelation. The fact that he liked Paul better seems incidental to me. I think he gives plenty of examples of Paul being overbearing and childish. But he was also the most committed to keeping the group going and therefore put in the most work, why people are still defensive about this stuff is beyond me.<br /><br />In conclusion, nice book by someone who was there for (part) of their historic run. Self-serving at times, interesting at other times, and telling us what we already know most of the time.

B00EOO55GW|3.0|Short answer: For me, NO. But for the mainstream media, YES.<br /><br />Still a very solid album, and is their most radio-friendly album to date.<br /><br />Being an A7X fan, I am accustomed to that distinct sound. Listen to their first 5 albums and you will hear things like church bells, piano, organs, dueling guitars, violins and complex melodies. In this album, those are missing.  By simplifying the song composition in HTTK, they lost that A7X sound that I have grown to love.<br /><br />Here's my track by track rating:<br />1) Shepherd of Fire [3/5]: Very upbeat intro track.  You can totally see this being played as a first song in a concert.<br />2) Hail to the King [3/5]: Obvious this is their first single.  A catchy song and will get a ton of radio play.<br />3) Doing Time [2/5]: Not a fan of this one. It sounded monotonous, as most \\"riff driven\\" songs tend to do.<br />4) This Means War [2/5]: Now this one is very monotonous, downright boring (listen to the verses!). My least favorite track.  Guitar solo's were good though so it got a bump to 2 stars.<br />5) Requiem [3/5]: Also got a bit monotonous at times.  Very old school sounding. Nothing wrong with old metal, but also nothing special on this one.  Only saving grace is during the bridge, where they flashed a bit of old A7X goth.<br />6) Crimson Day [3/5]: Decent ballad.  I know they've put out good slow tunes before, so this isn't a total surprise.  While it isn't \\"I Wont See You Tonight Part 1\\", its still very catchy.<br />7) Heretic [5/5]: My favorite track! This is one of two tracks that slightly resembled previous A7x albums, which is probably why I like it! Also as a bassist, it's always good to hear the bass track prominent in a track.  Great Job, Johnny Christ.<br />8) Coming Home [4/5]: Total headbang song.  They stuck to that old school metal sound.  Difference with this one, though, is that I can totally see myself pumping my fist, headbanging, when they play this live.<br />9) Planets [3/5]: The other \\"A7X sounding\\" track.  Still missing complexity but is the most drum-complex track of the album.<br />10) Acid Rain [3/5]: This feels like November Rain by GnR. It is quite catchy but didn't quite seem to fit this album.  May have fit Nightmare, since it sounds very much a tribute to The Rev.<br /><br />I have no doubt this will be a successful album, but it wasn't my favorite album either. I do recognize that in order to tread water in this (what seems like a deteriorating) music industry, a band must keep on evolving or it will drown... and that's what A7X is doing. While HTTK isn't what I was expecting, I'm at least comforted to know that A7X will stick around for a while. This album will definitely capture a bigger more mainstream audience, with its simplistic riff-driven sound. I now look forward to their tour this Fall/Winter and future albums to come. R.I.P. The Rev.

B00CPWTRAA|4.0|There have been few, if any, careers in opera to equal that of Placido Domingo. I first heard him in 1969, as Manrico in the new Met production of \\"il Trovatore\\", singing beside Leontyne Price, Grace Bumbry and Sherrill Milnes. He made his entrance at the end of Act 2 swinging in on a rope, the very image of youthful virility and impetuosity. He managed the high note at the end of \\"di quella pira\\", although he did not linger on it, and that was always his way, like most spintos, with high notes. He spent the next four decades and more at the very top of the opera world with a repertoire truly breathtaking in its range, including well-respected Wagner interpretations alongside the great Italian and French roles.  Here we are almost 45 years later, with a generous (70 minute) recital of extremely heavy, demanding Verdi roles, as a baritone. Among important tenors I can think of only a few who have sung successfully as baritones late in their career; Ramon Vinay comes to mind. Many tenors have begun as baritones, and Domingo might be thought to have done so since he began singing baritone in zarzuela as a very young man. There is much to appreciate here, especially his great energy and engagement with the dramatic requirements of the music. He is not afraid to make an ugly sound: the beginning of the Council Chamber scene from \\"Simon Boccangraa\\" is a shout of rage, and later his denunciation of Paolo is quite chilling. His lower voice has been developed very successfully; it sounds more baritonal, in fact, than many \\"real\\" Verdi baritones, who are often somewhat weak at the bottom of their range. He sings with enormous passion and truthfulness, a proud, still-strong old man who has nothing to lose and who will not go gently. There is often a pronounced beat, an inevitable loosening of vibrato, but never a wobble. He sounds better than any of the male singers in the supporting parts--in fact Machado is far less steady than Domingo in Don Carlos' prison cell. He is daring in his choice of repertoire, including the extremely exposed music from \\"Ernani\\" and the high-lying, treacherous tessitura of \\"il balen\\", with not entirely perfect success. The angry, vigorous characters are somewhat more comfortable than the contemplative ones. It would be foolish to expect real vocal freshness or consistent tonal beauty from him, from any singer, at 70, and those are not on offer. What you'll find here is complete fearlessness, a lifetime's hard-won craft and an indomitable passion. Verdi never wrote the King Lear he tried throughout much of his life to create, but the spirit of King Lear is Domingo's here. Whatever quibbles might be made, I think old Verdi would be proud.

0307887448|5.0|Wow.  Now this was a fantastic book.  From the beginning, I could not wait to get back to the book, from whatever it was that was tearing me away from it--friends, family, vacation... Must... Read... Book....<br /><br />I keep picking up books hoping that they'll actually transport me elsewhere -- take me to the land of the book and not left on my couch reading a book.  Where others have failed, Cline succeeded.<br /><br />SYNOPSIS<br />This book takes place in the the not-too-distant future, after the \\"Great Recession\\" has left the world a bleeding dump of what it was in the (now) past.  Trailer Parks have been built up into stacks, with RVs and mobile homes being stacked 15-20 homes high, most people have abandoned their vehicles because of the prohibitive cost of gasoline, food is a scarce resource, and fossil fuels have been burned up, leaving energy itself endangered.<br /><br />Yet, there is OASIS.  Originally designed as an \\"MMA\\" (multiple player video game), the OASIS quickly became a virtual reality for almost everyone in the world, with people \\"plugging in\\" as often and for as long as they could.  The creator of the OASIS - Jim Halliday - dies, leaving behind his fortune to whoever wins \\"the game\\" -- whoever collects the 3 keys and passes the 3 gates first.  For the next five years, no one finds even the first key... but then, it's on....<br /><br />BUT WHY'S IT GOOD?<br />Yeah it's hard to describe.  I'm not a gamer and didn't play most of the games mentioned in the book (of course I played pac man).  Halliday was born almost a decade before I was, so all of his nostalgia kind of just missed me.  I have seen several of the movies mentioned... but then again, there were a slew that I had not.  This book is not just nostalgia-crack and it's definitely not just for gamers.<br /><br />The brilliance of this book is that it's set in the not-too-distant future, realistic in many ways, with a quality of virtual reality that is probably just around the corner from today... so it's almost not even sci-fi or fantasy.  Yet through OASIS, the participant can do just about anything--visit planets described in books, be a wizard or a fairy, actually play a character in a movie, even go to school--anything someone can think of aside from the necessary bodily functions... of course, all for a cost (actual money).  So, at the beginning of the book, the main character, Wade Watts, doesn't have access to anything.  Essentially, just some kiddie video games, the public library, and school.  But he's smart, resourceful, and willing to devote serious amounts of time to the game.<br /><br />But the brilliance of this book is the relatability -- Wade Watts is some shy, dorky kid, who is insecure and only able to come out of his shell behind the safety of the virtual reality lens.  But somehow this shy, dorky, insecure kid is the hero of the book, and somehow you believe it and root for it.  And somehow maybe it feels a little bit like you.<br /><br />But the brilliance of this book is that it abounds with descriptions of games and movies that at least some of its readers (e.g., me) have never played, seen, or even heard of, and yet it's so. freaking. fascinating.  I just ate it all up.<br /><br />But the brilliance of this book is that, when I wasn't reading... I started to almost believe that some of what was happening in the book (the \\"future\\" stuff) was possible, happening, present.<br /><br />So yeah, I absolutely loved this book.  I always hope to read a book that takes me there and makes me believe that it's real, and makes me feel connected to the characters, and makes me actually care about the outcome.  And here it was.<br /><br />I *really* hope there's a movie someday!<br />FIVE out of five stars.<br /><br />**This and other other reviews can be found at AllBookReviewer.blogspot.comm today... so it's almost not even sci-fi or fantasy.  Yet through OASIS, the participant can do just about anything--visit planets described in books, be a wizard or a fairy, actually play a character in a movie, even go to school--anything someone can think of aside from the necessary bodily functions... of course, all for a cost (actual money).  So, at the beginning of the book, the main character, Wade Watts, doesn't have access to anything.  Essentially, just some kiddie video games, the public library, and school.  But he's smart, resourceful, and willing to devote serious amounts of time to the game.<br /><br />But the brilliance of this book is the relatability -- Wade Watts is some shy, dorky kid, who is insecure and only able to come out of his shell behind the safety of the virtual reality lens.  But somehow this shy, dorky, insecure kid is the hero of the book, and somehow you believe it and root for it.  And somehow maybe it feels a little bit like you.<br /><br />But the brilliance of this book is that it abounds with descriptions of games and movies that at least some of its readers (e.g., me) have never played, seen, or even heard of, and yet it's so. freaking. fascinating.  I just ate it all up.<br /><br />But the brilliance of this book is that, when I wasn't reading... I started to almost believe that some of what was happening in the book (the \\"future\\" stuff) was possible, happening, present.<br /><br />So yeah, I absolutely loved this book. 0385523394|4.0|Review based on ARC.<br /><br />This is one of those books that was entertaining, informative, and infuriating.  Piper Kerman wrote a memoir that reads almost like a diary.  This is both a compliment and a criticism.  The problem with the non-diary-diary format is that it was scattered, unfocused, and anecdotal.  The good thing about the non-diary-diary format, however, is that it was interesting, personal, and present.<br /><br />So, the story.  Kerman makes massively stupid and selfish decisions in her young life, as young ladies are wont to do, and years later, she begins to pay for it.  a little.  Her involvement in large-scale drug trafficking ultimately lands her in inside a minimum security federal prison, which she proceeds to detail in a manner that is, at turns, entertaining and lecturing.  There's not really much more to the plot:  she learns about being in prison, she learns about the people there, she learns about the impact of her actions.  Thus, a memoir.<br /><br />What works is Kerman's tone, most of the time.  She takes an equally amusing and reverent tone in describing her prison-mates and describes heartbreaking, enraging, and amusing stories alike.  Kerman's own appearance and naivety land her, fortunately, friends with some of the more powerful women in the prison and, to hear Kerman tell it, she herself ends up being one of the most popular prisoners to ever grace Danbury's walls (this is a literal exaggeration of the tone that many of the anecdotes presented -- she never, of course, ever states anything quite so ludicrous).<br /><br />Kerman flits between anecdotes, grand philosophical rants, political lectures, and summaries, which unorganized presentation was, at many points, annoying and disrupted the flow of the tale.  However, Kerman's voice was consistent throughout these variances, making the sudden switches more tolerable to read.<br /><br />Kerman mentions in passing that her year had its rough moments... but aside from dealing with her Grandmother's illness, stays far on the surface of these times.  Which leads the reader to believe that her time in Danbury wasn't so bad--that maybe she was just a \\"whiney privileged white girl.\\"  In reality, there were probably times when it was terrible for *her* (and not just one of the other girls there), and maybe she didn't want to relive those in her memoir.  Or maybe she didn't want to be too depressing for her readers.  Or maybe just being incapable of leaving the walls for such an extended period of time was rough.  She managed to remain a picky eater, looked years younger after LEAVING prison, worked out, got a lot of daytime, watched movies, had friends.... yeah, I know a lot of girls who'd kill for her crappy year.  But, as I say, maybe there was some stuff under the surface that she didn't divulge.<br /><br />All in all, the book gets THREE AND A HALF stars (bumped to 4 on non-half-star sites for its importance) because Kerman tells a good story and creates a few vivid characters (though a few others are glaringly 2-dimensional), and because the story she is telling and the impact it has had and can have is important.  Important!  For any person who knows no-one who has been to jail or prison (even a \\"swanky\\" minimum security prison like Danbury), or anyone who thinks that prisoners cannot be rehabilitated and/or do not have any good in them, this book is a MUST.<br /><br />For the rest of us who've had harder lives, I say go ahead and read it if it sounds interesting.  It certainly is not a bad book and, even though it's a bit disorganized, it tells an interesting story.<br /><br />**side note: I am both interested in watching the tv show to see some of the characters come to life, and adamantly opposed in case my fears that it has created caricatures of very real people's lives, pain, and struggles are justified.<br /><br />This and other reviews can be found at AllBookReviewer.blogspot.comher Grandmother's illness, stays far on the surface of these times. B00EB85AP2|3.0|Twenty years ago it was hard to find a recording of The Bells with top-notch Russian forces, although a very good one under Evgeny Svetlanov was available on Melodiya, and still is, in somewhat rough Soviet sound. Since then, the field has burgeoned, and Svetlanov even added a live performance on ICA that is superb in every way - it does full justice to Rachmaninov's choral masterpiece. There are a handful of other contenders in modern sound, including Noseda (a bit cautious), Bychkov (live, very idiomatic, with a particularly sensitive tenor), and Pletnev (fine all around, more refined than dramatic). As the lead reviewer notes, what's missing is an account from the most prominent Russian conductors based in the uK, Gergiev, Petrenko, and Jurowski. But we aren't suffering from lack of choice.<br /><br />Rattle's main advantage is twofold. The minor advantage is that he's paired The Bells with Rachmaninov's last orchestral work, the vibrant Symphonic Dances, in case a buyer wants both pieces together. On the other hand, collectors who love the Symphonic Dances are likely to possess two or three copies already (my favorite comes from Petrenko and the Royal LIverpool Phil. on Avie). Rattle's major advantage is the glorious Berlin Phil., which rarely records any substantial Russian music; you have to go back to the Karajan era to find a Tchaikovsky symphony.<br /><br />The Berliners come through as expected, so if that whets your appetite, Rattle's interpretations are refined and detailed. EMI's sound highlights the orchestra's beautiful soloists, and the Berlin Radio Chorus is resounding, although their Russian is unintelligible - no great drawback since that's the case on most of the other leading recordings. All three vocal soloists are excellent, the palm going to Mikhail Petrenko, a star from the Mariinsky Opera. Although she's in her early fifties, the Slovak soprano L'uba Organosova has retained much of her lovely lyric tone, and several of her rivals exhibit considerably more Slavic wobble.<br /><br />But it's not quite a case of \\"what's not to like?\\" Rattle misses a great deal in the Symphonic Dances. He underinflects every movement, preferring to proceed correctly and almost blandly. There isn't a hint of seduction or wooziness in the saxophone solo in the first dance; the sharp, etched-glass chords that open the second dance aren't accented. Rhythms are square instead of springy and dance-like. A momentary comparison with Petrenko shows up Rattle's silken refinement for what it is. If you already own a better recording, this one adds very little to the program, making the CD's total timing short shrift. (The most interesting pairing for The Bells come on the Noseda and live Svetlanov disc.)<br /><br />Overall, Rattle's tendency to be too civilized comes into play, either a little (The Bells) or a lot (Symphonic Dances). How you feel about this will probably determine how you feel about the recording as a whole. I found it hard to stay engaged all the way through.nsiderably more Slavic wobble.<br /><br />But it's not quite a case of \\"what's not to like?\\" Rattle misses a great deal in the Symphonic Dances. B00979CS50|5.0|Ever the musical chameleon, Neil Young never rests on his laurels, and always does exactly what he wants.  What he wants today apparently, is to get back to the feedback laden grungy sound he forged with his band Crazy Horse back in the 90's with albums like \\"Ragged Glory\\" and even earlier with gems like \\"Rust Never Sleeps\\".  This is a sprawling 2 disc extravaganza with Young at his heaviest no holds barred, mostly heavy stomping music that should appeal to anyone who likes the more rock oriented side of his catalouge. There are several tracks here that clock in at well over the 15 minute mark.  These are full of Young And Company's patented sloppy jams.  For many bands this would be a distorted mess, but Young has always had a way of making simple jams sound cool, and as always, Crazy Horse is the perfect foil to Young's meandering guitar exploits.  Most of the lyrics on this one seem to be a reflection of Neil's past and the generation that spawned him.  Yes, there is some repetitiveness to some of these tunes, and they probably could have been pared down a bit, but overall I think the lyrics and messages are poignant vintage Young.  Your enjoyment of this album will most likely depend on which side of Mr. Young you like.  There are no ballads or acoustic songs to be found here.  This is rock n roll as only Neil Young and Crazy Horse can pull off and I think it may be one of the best albums of his career.

B00DY951RQ|2.0|All great artists have relatively barren (or, if you prefer, fallow) periods--it's nothing to get strung out about, just something to acknowledge.  What this collection does is really confirm our sense that, yes, around 1970 Dylan was on sabbatical from most of his musical energy and talent.  The issue is a little confused because around 1967-68 there was sort of a fake dry-spell which turned out to be tremendously rich (The Basement Tapes & associated material--wish they'd released some of this).  But \\"Self Portrait\\" really was a pretty bad album.  There are a few great tracks (covers + \\"The Mighty Quinn\\"), and many weak ones.  At two disks, bulked out with ho-hum concert recordings, it seemed to be scraping for material.<br /><br />But there was more--and worse.  Which is where Columbia's revenge album, \\"Dylan,\\" came from.  In the years since, other bits and bobs have been dug up from the period--a few wonderful.  But now, a whole 35 more tracks?  What could these be?<br /><br />Mostly junk.  Not really awful junk--just stuff worse than what was actually released.  The terrific thing about Dylan bootlegs is that he occasionally left \\"Blind Willie McTell,\\" \\"She's Your Lover Now,\\" or \\"Positively Van Gogh\\" on the cutting room floor.  But this collection gives you the sense that this time round Dylan-as-editor was on top of the situation, even if Dylan-as-songwriter was not.  Sometimes the rejected version we find here is nice in its own right (\\"Went to See the Gypsy\\"), sometimes it is not (\\"Time Passes Slowly\\").  Often, it just seems redundant.  \\"I Threw It All Away,\\" is a sound-alike of the released version; \\"Alberta\\" is a good song that we already have in two different versions on \\"Self Portrait.\\"  All the songs marked \\"Without Overdubs\\" sound anti-climactic: the drums are the main things gone, and it makes you realize how much the drums did for a song like \\"Days of 49.\\"  And if you doubt that Dylan was in artistic decline in this period, just compare the ploddingversion of \\"House Carpenter\\" on this album with the harrowing take he did of the song in 1961.  In the middle are some OK surprises--\\"Annie's Going to...,\\" \\"Thirsty Boots.\\"<br /><br />All that said, it is an interesting collection to listen to if you're already interested in Dylan.  It fills out our picture of his career at a somewhat mysterious period of artistic retrofit and rebuilding--there's kind of a \\"back to the drawing board\\" feeling to this work.  ...And there is one really beautiful hidden gem: \\"Pretty Saro.\\"

0316228583|5.0|A Casual Vacancy is structured like a Victorian novel: many characters written in the omniscient point of view so that the author can slip in and out of the minds of each character. The book is complex and multilayerd. JKR begins with the death of Barry Fairbrother, a councilman in the small down of Pagford. His death causes ripples to move through the cauldron of pettiness that is Pagford revealing the truth behind the facades of many of the most prominent members of the community. This book comes the closest of any I've read in years of replicating the experience of being a psychotherapist, of learning what most lives are really about behind their appearances. JKR's psychological acumen is outstanding, especially when portraying the dynamics between prominent hypocritical parents and their mostly dramatically disturbed offspring. In the character of Simon Price, she presents the best depiction of a malignant narcissist I've ever read. The first 60-80 pages are difficult because the author presents many characters (reminiscent of Richard Russo) and the reader must work to keep them straight, but the labor involved is more than richly rewarded. I'm afraid that the books mixed reviews are coming from reviewers (even from some of our most prominent newspapers) who simply don't want to spend the time on complex novels that can't be speed read. God knows what these reviewers would have done with Dostoevsky or Tolstoy. They certainly lose patience with other brilliant authors - Murakami, for instance. Anyway, A Casual Vacancy is a brilliant novel, worthy of the woman who created the Potter series.
